Report issue for preceding element KEDA: Kubernetes-based Event Driven Autoscaling KEDA is an open-source Kubernetes operator enabling event-driven autoscaling for workloads using sources like Kafka, Prometheus, or AWS SQS ( KEDA, ) . It integrates with Kubernetes' Horizontal Pod Autoscaler (HPA) by exposing custom metrics and can scale workloads from zero to optimize resource use. Report issue for preceding element How It Works: Report issue for preceding element • Deployment: Runs as an operator, watching ScaledObjects that define scaling rules. Report issue for preceding element • Event Monitoring: Triggers scaling by evaluating event source metrics. Report issue for preceding element • HPA Integration: Feeds event metrics into HPA for scaling decisions. Report issue for preceding element • Scale-to-Zero: Frees resources by scaling to zero when idle. Report issue for preceding element Carbon-Aware KEDA Operator: An extension that uses real-time carbon intensity data (e.g., from WattTime or Electricity Map) to limit KEDA scaling. It introduces a custom resource CarbonAwareKedaScaler to set MaxReplicaCount, throttling workloads during high-carbon periods and increasing scale when carbon intensity is low. Report issue for preceding element CASA: A Framework for SLO- and Carbon-Aware Auto scaling and Scheduling in Serverless Cloud Computing This paper proposes a carbon emissions and Service Level Objectives(SLOs) aware container scheduling and auto-scaling framework called CASA, designed for serverless computing platforms. The framework aims to address the challenge of balancing carbon footprint minimization with performance maintenance in a Function-as-a-Service (FaaS) environment. Traditional energy-saving methods, such as shutting down idle containers, often result in increased latency due to cold starts, while methods that improve performance by preheating containers increase emissions. CASA achieves this balance by dynamically adjusting container scheduling and auto-scaling strategies based on carbon intensity and SLO constraints. Experimental evaluations show that CASA significantly reduces carbon emissions and service level violation rates compared to existing best practices ( qi2024casa, ) . Report issue for preceding element GreenWhisk: Emission-Aware Computing for Serverless Platform GreenWhisk is a carbon-aware serverless computing platform built on Apache OpenWhisk, designed to reduce the environmental impact of jobs execution in cloud environments. It addresses fluctuations in grid carbon intensity and renewable energy availability by integrating carbon-aware load balancing algorithms. GreenWhisk supports both grid-connected and grid-isolated modes, enabling transparent jobs scheduling based on real-time carbon and energy data without compromising performance. As a flexible infrastructure, the platform can seamlessly integrate various carbon-awareness strategies into serverless architectures ( serenari2024greenwhisk, ) . Report issue for preceding element 7.2.4. Carbon Emission Index Priority Scheduling Frameworks Report issue for preceding element Carbon Emission-Aware Job Scheduling for Kubernetes Deployments Report issue for preceding element The goal of this paper is to present a practical approach to reducing carbon emissions in Kubernetes by smartly scheduling workloads. The core idea is to use history energy statistics to identify times when energy has less carbon footprints and shift non-critical tasks to those periods. We make sure this doesn't affect the performance of critical jobs, so services stay reliable ( piontek2024carbon, ) . Report issue for preceding element The key contributions include: Report issue for preceding element • A carbon emission-aware scheduling algorithm that integrates seamlessly with Kubernetes by adapting job submission times based on historical carbon intensity trends. Report issue for preceding element • A priority-aware mechanism to distinguish between critical and delay-tolerant jobs, ensuring that only flexible workloads are shifted to reduce emissions. Report issue for preceding element • Demonstration of substantial carbon footprint reduction with negligible impact on job performance in practical Kubernetes clusters. Report issue for preceding element This work shows how Kubernetes can be adapted to make cloud computing greener. By taking into account periods of lower energy consumption, we can run workloads more sustainably without sacrificing speed or system availability. Report issue for preceding element CASPER: Carbon-Aware Scheduling and Provisioning for Distributed Web Services CASPER reduces the carbon footprint of geographically distributed web services by scheduling requests in regions with lower carbon intensity, while meeting service-level latency constraints ( Souza_2023, ) . Deployed in Kubernetes, CASPER consists of: Report issue for preceding element • Carbon-Aware Provisioner (CAP): Optimizes the number and location of active servers to minimize emissions while satisfying latency SLOs. It formulates a multi-objective problem using estimated carbon intensities and user request data, ignoring resource and communication constraints except server count. Report issue for preceding element • Carbon-Aware Scheduler (CAS): Acts as a load balancer, distributing user requests across regions per CAP's provisioning plan, and schedules requests not covered by CAP. Report issue for preceding element Experiments show up to 70% energy savings with minimal performance impact when CASPER is integrated with Kubernetes. Report issue for preceding element PCAPS: Carbon- and Precedence-Aware Scheduling for Data Processing Clusters This paper explores how to address carbon-aware scheduling challenges in large-scale data processing tasks where task dependencies exist. It introduces PCAPS, a scheduler that combines time-varying carbon intensity data with task priorities to avoid delaying critical upstream tasks that could block the entire processing pipeline. PCAPS integrates with machine learning based schedulers and provides a configurable trade-off between minimizing carbon emissions and maintaining task completion times. The authors also propose CAP, a generic wrapper for carbon-neutral schedulers that inherits PCAPS's resource scheduling strategies. Experiments on a 100-node Kubernetes Spark cluster demonstrate that PCAPS can reduce carbon emissions by up to 32.9% while maintaining overall scheduling efficiency ( lechowicz2025pcaps, ) . Report issue for preceding element Operating Cloud Applications Under a Carbon Budget This paper proposes a carbon-aware deployment strategy for long-running microservice-based cloud applications that maintains application availability and performance under all conditions. Unlike traditional carbon-aware scheduling methods for batch tasks, this method operates on an hourly carbon budget and dynamically adjusts application configurations (including microservice versions and horizontal scaling) to optimize user quality of experience(QoE) and revenue while meeting environmental constraints. The authors propose an optimization algorithm that selects the most appropriate deployment configuration based on workload, carbon intensity, and budget, and validate its effectiveness through a flight booking application. The proposed method outperforms the baseline in terms of QoE and revenue while meeting the carbon budget, demonstrating its practical application value in real-world dynamic environments ( espinosa2024optimizing, ) . Report issue for preceding element U-DUCT: Uncertainty-aware Dynamic Unified Carbon Modeling Tool for Datacenter Scheduling The U-DUCT framework proposes an uncertain, dynamic, and unified carbon emissions modeling tool designed to comprehensively assess carbon emissions in data centers during both the design phase and operational phase. Unlike previous tools, U-DUCT not only considers computing servers but also incorporates the contributions of often-overlooked components such as storage devices and switches. Additionally, the framework accounts for variability and uncertainty in hardware and software characteristics, which can impact embedded carbon emissions and operational carbon emissions. Through this holistic and runtime-aware model, U-DUCT has identified new opportunities for emissions reduction in the actual computational workloads of data center components ( guan2024u, ) . Report issue for preceding element A Green Cloud-Based Framework for Energy Efficient Task Scheduling Using Carbon Intensity Data for Heterogeneous Cloud Servers Report issue for preceding element This paper proposes a cloud-based scheduling framework that integrates real-time carbon intensity data to optimize the execution of energy intensive tasks in cloud data centers. The framework applied Kubernetes, AWS services, and containerized workloads to dynamically reschedule jobs with high energy consumptions based on the changes of regional carbon intensity. It results reducing operational carbon emissions without compromising performance. The framework emphasizes automation, scalability, and compatibility with major cloud providers, supporting sustainable practices aligned with the United Nations' Sustainable Development Goals. The system enhances energy efficiency through intelligent workload management to address the growing energy demands of artificial intelligence and machine learning applications ( beena2025green, ) . Report issue for preceding element LinTS: Carbon-Aware Temporal Data Transfer Scheduling Across Cloud Data centers LinTS is a carbon-aware scheduling framework designed to reduce the environmental impact of inter-data center communication, which is the primary source of carbon emissions in cloud computing. By leveraging the temporal variation in carbon intensity across different data centers, LinTS optimizes data transmission scheduling to ensure that transfers occur during low-carbon periods while meeting all transmission deadlines. The system outperforms traditional heuristic algorithms through intelligent scaling and transmission decisions, reducing carbon emissions by up to 66% compared to worst-case scenarios and by 15% compared to state-of-the-art baselines. LinTS demonstrates how to effectively utilize time-based carbon data to enhance the sustainability of cloud infrastructure without compromising operational constraints ( rodrigues2025carbon, ) . Report issue for preceding elementChallenges and Research Opportunities Report issue for preceding element 8.1. GPU Scheduler Report issue for preceding element Enhancing Kubernetes scheduling with AI, particularly for GPU resource management, is a promising area. Song proposes dividing physical GPUs into multiple virtual GPUs to boost cluster utilization by 10% ( song2018gaia, ) . Thinakaran develops a Kubernetes scheduler leveraging real-time GPU usage metrics to dynamically allocate GPU resources ( thinakaran2019kube, ) . However, GPU scheduling in Kubernetes remains underexplored, with efficient container-level resource management still needing advancement ( carrion2022kubernetes, ) . Report issue for preceding element SCI Data and Location-Shifting Improving software carbon intensity (SCI) data is essential. Collecting energy data for virtual machines is harder than for bare metal. Additionally, configuring carbon intensity based on both time and location could offer more effective scheduling opportunities. Report issue for preceding element 8.2. Algorithm Trade-offs Report issue for preceding element Current schedulers prioritize resource utilization but often ignore power usage efficiency (PUE), a key metric for sustainability. Reducing carbon intensity may require trade-offs, such as choosing between low-latency, high-carbon regions and high-latency, low-carbon ones. Future algorithms must account for the dynamic nature of both workloads and energy profiles. Report issue for preceding element 8.3. Microservices Scheduling Report issue for preceding element Kubernetes faces challenges in managing microservices and modeled workloads. Pod-level scheduling can be inefficient for microservices. Gang scheduling, which groups related pods, can improve coordination. Meanwhile, workload prediction remains underdeveloped and is vital for efficient scheduling under dynamic demands. Report issue for preceding element 8.4. GPU Power Capping for Sustainable AI Report issue for preceding element GPU power capping can improve energy efficiency in HPC. A study on MIT SuperCloud showed that applying a 60% power cap significantly reduced power consumption and GPU temperatures with minimal performance impact. However, care is needed to avoid triggering excess jobs that offset energy savings. Integrating such capping techniques into Kubernetes could benefit sustainable AI workloads ( zhao2023sustainable, ) . Report issue for preceding element 8.5. Data Center Optimization Report issue for preceding element Kubernetes can reduce carbon intensity through component impact measurement, task rescheduling, and carbon-aware algorithms. Baris shows that eco-friendly UPS batteries lower peak power costs ( baris, ) , while Kontorinis finds no performance loss from such substitutions ( kontorinis2012managing, ) . Kuroda enhances power systems by reducing AC-to-DC conversions ( kuroda2013high, ) . Hancock recommends cold-region data centers (e.g., Iceland) for cooling benefits ( HancockIceland, ) . Future modeling should examine how power delivery and consumption influence carbon intensity ( datacentersreview, ) . Report issue for preceding element 8.6. Federated Learning Report issue for preceding element One of the main challenges facing FL algorithms is the heterogeneity of client data. For example, different types of data may not be independently and identically distributed (non-IID) in terms of distribution, volume, or quality. For instance, some devices may only see digits 0 and 1 with skewed frequencies ( nonIIDexplain, ) , affecting aggregation and model convergence. Report issue for preceding element This problem worsens when FL is deployed on edge devices using green energy (e.g., wind turbines or sensors), which have variable data volumes due to memory, load, and location differences ( brecko2022federated, ; xie2022improving, ) . Limited bandwidth and intermittent connectivity further disrupt timely model updates ( kang2020reliable, ) . Designing robust aggregation strategies under these constraints is an emerging research trend ( abreha2022federated, ) . Report issue for preceding elementConclusion Report issue for preceding element This report discusses the shift in cloud computing from virtualization to containerization and the energy consumption challenges associated with these technologies, with a focus on the industry-standard container orchestration engine, Kubernetes. This work investigates not only the default scheduling and auto-scaling features of Kubernetes but also advanced hardware and software optimizations that can improve energy efficiency and carbon awareness in large scale data center operations. In our extensive literature review, we discuss the optimizations that have been applied to the Kubernetes scheduler for temporal and spatial shifting of workloads which reduces the environmental impact of workload execution. This report investigates the use of state-of-the-art machine learning techniques such as deep reinforcement learning and federated learning for optimized workload scheduling in Kubernetes. We emphasize enhancing carbon efficiency in federated learning workloads within data centers, while also leveraging federated learning to optimize the scheduling of distributed workloads without breaking data privacy by training models in a decentralized manner. The analysis of the challenges and research opportunities highlights that future research in this direction necessitates close cooperation between the government and enterprises, universities and other organizations. The development of reasonable standards and norms will also help promote the promotion and application of this technology. Describe the issue below: Description: Submit without GitHub Submit in GitHub Report Issue for Selection Generated by L A T E xml Instructions for reporting errors We are continuing to improve HTML versions of papers, and your feedback helps enhance accessibility and mobile support. To report errors in the HTML that will help us improve conversion and rendering, choose any of the methods listed below: Click the "Report Issue" button. Open a report feedback form via keyboard, use " Ctrl + ?". Make a text selection and click the "Report Issue for Selection" button near your cursor. You can use Alt+Y to toggle on and Alt+Shift+Y to toggle off accessible reporting links at each section. Our team has already identified the following issues. We appreciate your time reviewing and reporting rendering errors we may not have found yet. Your efforts will help us improve the HTML versions for all readers, because disability should not be a barrier to accessing research. Thank you for your continued support in championing open access for all. Have a free development cycle? Help support accessibility at arXiv! Our collaborators at LaTeXML maintain a list of packages that need conversion, and welcome developer contributions.

Namespace access Organization access tokens Home / Manuals / Docker Engine / Swarm mode / Administer and maintain a swarm of Docker Engines Administer and maintain a swarm of Docker Engines Ask Gordon Copy Markdown View Markdown Table of contents Operate manager nodes in a swarm Maintain the quorum of managers Configure the manager to advertise on a static IP address Add manager nodes for fault tolerance Distribute manager nodes Run manager-only nodes Add worker nodes for load balancing Monitor swarm health Troubleshoot a manager node Forcibly remove a node Back up the swarm Recover from disaster Restore from a backup Recover from losing the quorum Force the swarm to rebalance When you run a swarm of Docker Engines, manager nodes are the key components for managing the swarm and storing the swarm state. It is important to understand some key features of manager nodes to properly deploy and maintain the swarm. Refer to How nodes work for a brief overview of Docker Swarm mode and the difference between manager and worker nodes. Operate manager nodes in a swarm Swarm manager nodes use the Raft Consensus Algorithm to manage the swarm state. You only need to understand some general concepts of Raft in order to manage a swarm. There is no limit on the number of manager nodes. The decision about how many manager nodes to implement is a trade-off between performance and fault-tolerance. Adding manager nodes to a swarm makes the swarm more fault-tolerant. However, additional manager nodes reduce write performance because more nodes must acknowledge proposals to update the swarm state. This means more network round-trip traffic. Raft requires a majority of managers, also called the quorum, to agree on proposed updates to the swarm, such as node additions or removals. Membership operations are subject to the same constraints as state replication. Maintain the quorum of managers If the swarm loses the quorum of managers, the swarm cannot perform management tasks. If your swarm has multiple managers, always have more than two. To maintain quorum, a majority of managers must be available. An odd number of managers is recommended, because the next even number does not make the quorum easier to keep. For instance, whether you have 3 or 4 managers, you can still only lose 1 manager and maintain the quorum. If you have 5 or 6 managers, you can still only lose two. Even if a swarm loses the quorum of managers, swarm tasks on existing worker nodes continue to run. However, swarm nodes cannot be added, updated, or removed, and new or existing tasks cannot be started, stopped, moved, or updated. See Recovering from losing the quorum for troubleshooting steps if you do lose the quorum of managers. Configure the manager to advertise on a static IP address When initiating a swarm, you must specify the --advertise-addr flag to advertise your address to other manager nodes in the swarm. For more information, see Run Docker Engine in swarm mode. Because manager nodes are meant to be a stable component of the infrastructure, you should use a fixed IP address for the advertise address to prevent the swarm from becoming unstable on machine reboot. If the whole swarm restarts and every manager node subsequently gets a new IP address, there is no way for any node to contact an existing manager. Therefore the swarm is hung while nodes try to contact one another at their old IP addresses. Dynamic IP addresses are OK for worker nodes. Add manager nodes for fault tolerance You should maintain an odd number of managers in the swarm to support manager node failures. Having an odd number of managers ensures that during a network partition, there is a higher chance that the quorum remains available to process requests if the network is partitioned into two sets. Keeping the quorum is not guaranteed if you encounter more than two network partitions. For example, in a swarm with 5 nodes, if you lose 3 nodes, you don't have a quorum. Therefore you can't add or remove nodes until you recover one of the unavailable manager nodes or recover the swarm with disaster recovery commands. See Recover from disaster. While it is possible to scale a swarm down to a single manager node, it is impossible to demote the last manager node. This ensures you maintain access to the swarm and that the swarm can still process requests. Scaling down to a single manager is an unsafe operation and is not recommended. If the last node leaves the swarm unexpectedly during the demote operation, the swarm becomes unavailable until you reboot the node or restart with --force-new-cluster . You manage swarm membership with the docker swarm and docker node subsystems. Refer to Add nodes to a swarm for more information on how to add worker nodes and promote a worker node to be a manager. Distribute manager nodes In addition to maintaining an odd number of manager nodes, pay attention to datacenter topology when placing managers. For optimal fault-tolerance, distribute manager nodes across a minimum of 3 availability-zones to support failures of an entire set of machines or common maintenance scenarios. If you suffer a failure in any of those zones, the swarm should maintain the quorum of manager nodes available to process requests and rebalance workloads. Run manager-only nodes By default manager nodes also act as a worker nodes. This means the scheduler can assign tasks to a manager node. For small and non-critical swarms assigning tasks to managers is relatively low-risk as long as you schedule services using resource constraints for cpu and memory. However, because manager nodes use the Raft consensus algorithm to replicate data in a consistent way, they are sensitive to resource starvation. You should isolate managers in your swarm from processes that might block swarm operations like swarm heartbeat or leader elections. To avoid interference with manager node operation, you can drain manager nodes to make them unavailable as worker nodes: When you drain a node, the scheduler reassigns any tasks running on the node to other available worker nodes in the swarm. It also prevents the scheduler from assigning tasks to the node. Add worker nodes for load balancing Add nodes to the swarm to balance your swarm's load. Replicated service tasks are distributed across the swarm as evenly as possible over time, as long as the worker nodes are matched to the requirements of the services. When limiting a service to run on only specific types of nodes, such as nodes with a specific number of CPUs or amount of memory, remember that worker nodes that do not meet these requirements cannot run these tasks. Monitor swarm health You can monitor the health of manager nodes by querying the docker nodes API in JSON format through the /nodes HTTP endpoint. Refer to the nodes API documentation for more information. From the command line, run docker node inspect  to query the nodes. For instance, to query the reachability of the node as a manager: To query the status of the node as a worker that accept tasks: From those commands, we can see that manager1 is both at the status reachable as a manager and ready as a worker. An unreachable health status means that this particular manager node is unreachable from other manager nodes. In this case you need to take action to restore the unreachable manager: Restart the daemon and see if the manager comes back as reachable. Reboot the machine. If neither restarting nor rebooting works, you should add another manager node or promote a worker to be a manager node. You also need to cleanly remove the failed node entry from the manager set with docker node demote  and docker node rm  . Alternatively you can also get an overview of the swarm health from a manager node with docker node ls : Troubleshoot a manager node You should never restart a manager node by copying the raft directory from another node. The data directory is unique to a node ID. A node can only use a node ID once to join the swarm. The node ID space should be globally unique. To cleanly re-join a manager node to a cluster: Demote the node to a worker using docker node demote  . Remove the node from the swarm using docker node rm  . Re-join the node to the swarm with a fresh state using docker swarm join . For more information on joining a manager node to a swarm, refer to Join nodes to a swarm. Forcibly remove a node In most cases, you should shut down a node before removing it from a swarm with the docker node rm command. If a node becomes unreachable, unresponsive, or compromised you can forcefully remove the node without shutting it down by passing the --force flag. For instance, if node9 becomes compromised: Before you forcefully remove a manager node, you must first demote it to the worker role. Make sure that you always have an odd number of manager nodes if you demote or remove a manager. Back up the swarm Docker manager nodes store the swarm state and manager logs in the /var/lib/docker/swarm/ directory. This data includes the keys used to encrypt the Raft logs. Without these keys, you cannot restore the swarm. You can back up the swarm using any manager. Use the following procedure. If the swarm has auto-lock enabled, you need the unlock key to restore the swarm from backup. Retrieve the unlock key if necessary and store it in a safe location. If you are unsure, read Lock your swarm to protect its encryption key. Stop Docker on the manager before backing up the data, so that no data is being changed during the backup. It is possible to take a backup while the manager is running (a "hot" backup), but this is not recommended and your results are less predictable when restoring. While the manager is down, other nodes continue generating swarm data that is not part of this backup. Note Be sure to maintain the quorum of swarm managers. During the time that a manager is shut down, your swarm is more vulnerable to losing the quorum if further nodes are lost. The number of managers you run is a trade-off. If you regularly take down managers to do backups, consider running a five manager swarm, so that you can lose an additional manager while the backup is running, without disrupting your services. Back up the entire /var/lib/docker/swarm directory. Restart the manager. To restore, see Restore from a backup. Recover from disaster Restore from a backup After backing up the swarm as described in Back up the swarm, use the following procedure to restore the data to a new swarm. Shut down Docker on the target host machine for the restored swarm. Remove the contents of the /var/lib/docker/swarm directory on the new swarm. Restore the /var/lib/docker/swarm directory with the contents of the backup. Note The new node uses the same encryption key for on-disk storage as the old one. It is not possible to change the on-disk storage encryption keys at this time. In the case of a swarm with auto-lock enabled, the unlock key is also the same as on the old swarm, and the unlock key is needed to restore the swarm. Start Docker on the new node. Unlock the swarm if necessary. Re-initialize the swarm using the following command, so that this node does not attempt to connect to nodes that were part of the old swarm, and presumably no longer exist. Verify that the state of the swarm is as expected. This may include application-specific tests or simply checking the output of docker service ls to be sure that all expected services are present. If you use auto-lock, rotate the unlock key. Add manager and worker nodes to bring your new swarm up to operating capacity. Reinstate your previous backup regimen on the new swarm. Recover from losing the quorum Swarm is resilient to failures and can recover from any number of temporary node failures (machine reboots or crash with restart) or other transient errors. However, a swarm cannot automatically recover if it loses a quorum. Tasks on existing worker nodes continue to run, but administrative tasks are not possible, including scaling or updating services and joining or removing nodes from the swarm. The best way to recover is to bring the missing manager nodes back online. If that is not possible, continue reading for some options for recovering your swarm. In a swarm of N managers, a quorum (a majority) of manager nodes must always be available. For example, in a swarm with five managers, a minimum of three must be operational and in communication with each other. In other words, the swarm can tolerate up to (N-1)/2 permanent failures beyond which requests involving swarm management cannot be processed. These types of failures include data corruption or hardware failures. If you lose the quorum of managers, you cannot administer the swarm. If you have lost the quorum and you attempt to perform any management operation on the swarm, an error occurs: The best way to recover from losing the quorum is to bring the failed nodes back online. If you can't do that, the only way to recover from this state is to use the --force-new-cluster action from a manager node. This removes all managers except the manager the command was run from. The quorum is achieved because there is now only one manager. Promote nodes to be managers until you have the desired number of managers. From the node to recover, run: When you run the docker swarm init command with the --force-new-cluster flag, the Docker Engine where you run the command becomes the manager node of a single-node swarm which is capable of managing and running services. The manager has all the previous information about services and tasks, worker nodes are still part of the swarm, and services are still running. You need to add or re-add manager nodes to achieve your previous task distribution and ensure that you have enough managers to maintain high availability and prevent losing the quorum. Force the swarm to rebalance Generally, you do not need to force the swarm to rebalance its tasks. When you add a new node to a swarm, or a node reconnects to the swarm after a period of unavailability, the swarm does not automatically give a workload to the idle node. This is a design decision. If the swarm periodically shifted tasks to different nodes for the sake of balance, the clients using those tasks would be disrupted. The goal is to avoid disrupting running services for the sake of balance across the swarm. When new tasks start, or when a node with running tasks becomes unavailable, those tasks are given to less busy nodes. The goal is eventual balance, with minimal disruption to the end user. You can use the --force or -f flag with the docker service update command to force the service to redistribute its tasks across the available worker nodes. This causes the service tasks to restart. Client applications may be disrupted. If you have configured it, your service uses a rolling update. If you use an earlier version and you want to achieve an even balance of load across workers and don't mind disrupting running tasks, you can force your swarm to re-balance by temporarily scaling the service upward. Use docker service inspect --pretty  to see the configured scale of a service. When you use docker service scale , the nodes with the lowest number of tasks are targeted to receive the new workloads. There may be multiple under-loaded nodes in your swarm. You may need to scale the service up by modest increments a few times to achieve the balance you want across all the nodes. When the load is balanced to your satisfaction, you can scale the service back down to the original scale. You can use docker service ps to assess the current balance of your service across nodes. See also docker service scale and docker service ps . Source: Architectural Paradigms in Container Orchestration: A Comparative Engineering Study of Scheduling, Clustering, and Scaling MechanicsType: Pasted TextArchitectural Paradigms in Container Orchestration: A Comparative Engineering Study of Scheduling, Clustering, and Scaling MechanicsThe evolution of distributed computing has made container orchestration the core foundation of modern infrastructure engineering.[1, 2] At its root, virtualization abstracts physical hardware by sharing the host operating system kernel to package software binaries and their dependencies into lightweight, standalone compute units.[1, 3] To manage these units at scale, orchestration systems automate scheduling, clustering, and dynamic resource allocation.[1, 4]However, modern systems encounter a fundamental challenge: while default orchestration frameworks excel at optimizing initial container placement on static clusters, they are rarely designed to natively autoscale physical cluster sizes or calculate elastic cloud pricing model variations.[3, 4] These dynamic cluster-size adjustments are typically offloaded to external platform-level auto-scaling frameworks.[3, 4]This report provides a detailed architectural evaluation of the three dominant container orchestration paradigms: Kubernetes, representing the highly extensible and complex enterprise cloud-native standard [5, 6]; HashiCorp Nomad, representing a lightweight, high-performance, multi-workload scheduler [5, 7]; and Docker Swarm mode, representing a zero-overhead, simple, compose-based clustering engine.[5, 8]Kubernetes: The Cloud-Native Enterprise Platform Kubernetes operates as a highly resilient, declarative, API-driven platform designed to decouple microservices from physical infrastructure.[4, 6, 9] By formalizing state abstractions, Kubernetes automates application lifecycles across large compute pools.[6, 9] 1.1. Pods, Services, and Networking Evolution (Ingress to Gateway API) The core unit of Kubernetes scheduling is the Pod, which represents a single instance of a running process and encapsulates one or more co-located containers sharing storage, network namespaces, and a single loopback interface.[3, 9] Because Pods are ephemeral and undergo constant replication, termination, and host migration, the platform uses Services to establish logical, stable Layer 4 network endpoints.[9, 10] A Service acts as a bridge, utilizing internal DNS and network proxy routing rules to resolve the dynamic IP addresses of underlying backend containers.[9, 10] For external traffic management, the cluster historically relied on the Ingress resource, which maps incoming HTTP and HTTPS traffic (Layer 7) to internal Services based on hostnames or URL paths.[9, 11] While Ingress abstracts basic edge routing, it suffers from severe architectural limitations in complex, multi-tenant environments.[9, 12] Ingress relies on a monolithic resource model where infrastructure configurations and application-level routing rules are combined into a single file, preventing role separation between platform operators and application developers.[12] Furthermore, advanced Layer 7 traffic management capabilities—such as header-based routing, request/response manipulation, and weighted traffic splitting for canary deployments—are not supported natively within the standard Ingress specification.[11, 12] Implementing these features requires vendor-specific, non-portable annotations that differ across ingress controller implementations (e.g., NGINX, Traefik, HAProxy), leading to configuration drift and vendor lock-in.[11, 12] To resolve these limitations, the Kubernetes Special Interest Group (SIG) Network developed the Gateway API.[9, 13, 14] This next-generation networking framework decomposes traffic routing into modular, role-oriented Custom Resource Definitions (CRDs) that represent distinct organizational domains.[12, 13, 14] The historical progression and adoption timeline of the Gateway API demonstrate its growing dominance in cloud-native routing: The Gateway API separates cluster operations into three distinct organizational roles: Cluster Admin: Installs the controller implementation and defines the cluster-wide template via a GatewayClass resource.[12, 13, 14] Platform Team: Provisions the entry-point load balancer or proxy instance via a Gateway resource, configuring listeners, ports, protocols, TLS termination, and allowed namespaces.[12, 13, 14] Application Developer: Deploys protocol-specific Route resources—such as HTTPRoute or GRPCRoute—attaching them to existing Gateways to manage application paths without requiring cluster-level network permissions.[12, 13, 14] This role division is illustrated below: As of 2026, core resources—including GatewayClass, Gateway, HTTPRoute, GRPCRoute, TLSRoute, and ReferenceGrant—are generally available and production-ready, while lower-level Layer 4 routing resources like TCPRoute and UDPRoute remain experimental.[12] Crucially, the Gateway API also supports secure cross-namespace routing.[12, 14] Using ReferenceGrant objects, a Gateway in an administrative namespace can route traffic to Route resources or backend Services located in developer-restricted namespaces, allowing safe infrastructure sharing without direct team coordination.[12, 14] 1.2. The Operator Pattern and the Reconciliation Loop The architecture of Kubernetes relies on its declarative API, which can be extended via Custom Resource Definitions (CRDs).[15] Registering a CRD teaches the API server to validate and store custom schemas.[15, 16] When paired with a custom controller, this configuration implements the Operator Pattern.[15] The operational core of every operator is the reconciliation loop.[17] This loop continuously monitors the cluster, observing the desired state defined in the custom resource's spec and comparing it to the actual state of the cluster.[15, 16, 17] If a mismatch is identified, the controller executes API requests to resolve the drift, subsequently updating the resource's status subfield to reflect the outcome.[15, 16, 17] The engine driving this loop is provided by the controller-runtime library, which relies on informers and work queues.[17] Informers watch the API server for changes to the custom resource and its child objects, updating a local cache and passing event keys into a rate-limited work queue.[17, 18] The reconciler pulls items from the work queue to run the Reconcile function.[17, 18] If the operation encounters an error, the key is returned to the queue to be retried using exponential backoff.[15, 17] An operator must follow several architectural best practices: Controller Isolation: A controller should manage a single kind of custom resource to optimize scalability, ensure error isolation, and prevent performance bottlenecks.[19] Idempotency: The reconciliation logic must be state-agnostic and safe to run repeatedly, ensuring that executing the loop multiple times with the same input produces the same output without creating duplicate resources.[15, 17, 19] Owner References: Controllers must attach Owner References to child resources using helper functions like ctrl.SetControllerReference.[17] This binds the child object's lifecycle to the custom resource, allowing the Kubernetes garbage collector to automatically clean up all associated child resources when the parent is deleted.[17] Standardized Logging: Operator logging must adhere to structured formats to support aggregation in production.[19] The community guidelines recommend starting logs with a capital letter, omitting a trailing period, using active voice, applying past tense for completed actions, and explicitly stating the object type in a structured key-value format.[19] 1.3. Scheduling Mechanics and Advanced Bin-Packing The kube-scheduler assigns pending Pods to viable nodes through a two-phase pipeline: Filtering and Scoring.[1, 20] During the filtering phase, the scheduler evaluates active constraints using plugins like PodFitsResources, NodeAffinity, TaintsAndTolerations, and VolumeBinding to compile a list of feasible nodes.[1, 20] During the scoring phase, the scheduler ranks the remaining nodes using a configured scoring strategy to select the optimal host.[20] Three scoring strategies within the NodeResourcesFit plugin govern resource bin-packing and cluster utilization: The operational characteristics and trade-offs of these scoring strategies are detailed below: LeastAllocated Strategy: This strategy scores nodes higher when they have more unallocated resource capacity, spreading workloads across the cluster.[20] It ensures ample headroom on all nodes, minimizes the risk of noisy-neighbor resource contention, and is helpful when workload request sizing is still unstable.[20] However, it increases resource fragmentation, leading to many partially utilized nodes and preventing nodes from emptying completely, which hinders cluster scale-down operations.[20] Organizations using this strategy should track node counts, average requests-based utilization, and the percentage of nodes falling below the scale-down threshold.[20] MostAllocated Strategy: This strategy drives dense resource bin-packing by scoring nodes higher when they have higher resource allocations, filling existing nodes first before utilizing new ones.[20, 21] While this maximizes utilization and facilitates node scale-down (reducing cloud infrastructure costs), it introduces significant operational risks.[20] If a node is tightly packed and workloads experience sudden resource spikes that exceed their requests, the node can face CPU throttling, memory pressure, and out-of-memory (OOM) evictions.[20] To mitigate these issues, administrators must implement guardrails, including strict resource limits, Pod Disruption Budgets (PDBs), priority/preemption rules, and Horizontal Pod Autoscalers (HPAs).[20] RequestedToCapacityRatio Strategy: This strategy allows administrators to specify a custom scoring curve (or shape) that maps resource utilization percentages to specific scores.[20, 21] By defining a linear curve (such as 0% utilization scoring 0, rising to 100% utilization scoring 10), it can function as a bin-packer.[20, 21] Crucially, it can also weight extended resources (such as specialized network interfaces or GPUs like intel.com/foo and intel.com/bar with a weight of 3 compared to 1 for CPU/Memory) to prioritize the packing of scarce hardware.[20, 21] Despite its configuration options, the default Kubernetes scheduler has limitations.[1] It operates in a reactive, pod-by-pod fashion rather than performing global optimizations across job queues.[1] It lacks adaptive learning capabilities, is primarily resource-centric (focusing on CPU/memory rather than energy consumption or carbon footprint), and uses random tie-breaking when multiple nodes return identical scores, which can lead to inconsistent scheduling behavior.[1]
HashiCorp Nomad: Minimalist, Multi-Workload Orchestration HashiCorp Nomad represents a distinct architectural philosophy, prioritizing simplicity, operational efficiency, and multi-workload flexibility.[5, 22] Designed as a single, lightweight binary, Nomad acts as a high-throughput scheduler capable of orchestrating both containerized and non-containerized applications.[7, 22] 2.1. Single-Binary Simplicity and Operational Topography Unlike the multi-component control plane of Kubernetes, Nomad is deployed as a single compiled Go binary that runs as a lightweight daemon, operating either in server mode (coordinating state) or client mode (executing tasks).[5, 7, 22] This single-binary architecture reduces setup, layout, and clustering maintenance overhead.[5, 7, 22] The clustering model of Nomad organizes state management and scheduling around four primary primitives: Jobs: The declarative user manifest defining the desired state, task configurations, and constraints.[23] Nodes: The physical or virtual compute hosts executing client daemons.[23] Allocations: The runtime binding of a specific job's task groups to a designated node.[23] Evaluations: The decision-making events triggered by cluster state changes.[23] 2.2. Centralized Scheduling Pipeline and Optimistic Concurrency When the cluster state changes—such as a new job submission, node failure, or deployment update—Nomad generates a pending Evaluation.[23] This Evaluation is enqueued in the central Evaluation Broker, which runs on the leader server and handles priority ordering and at-least-once delivery.[23] Nomad servers run scheduling workers (defaulting to one per CPU core) that pull Evaluations from the broker and invoke the appropriate scheduler (Service, Batch, System, Sysbatch, or Core).[23] Schedulers run Evaluations through a two-phase pipeline: Feasibility Checking: The scheduler filters nodes based on constraints like datacenter target matching, driver availability, node health, and explicit job constraints.[23] Ranking: The scheduler scores feasible nodes, utilizing bin-packing and job anti-affinity rules to distribute task groups across separate fault domains, aiming to reduce the risk of correlated node failures.[23] To maintain high throughput, Nomad uses an optimistic concurrency model.[23] Multiple scheduling workers evaluate changes in parallel without locking the state database or reserving resources.[23] Once a worker formulates an allocation plan, it submits it to the leader's Plan Queue.[23] The leader serializes plans, performing verification checks to prevent resource oversubscription.[23] If a conflict occurs, the leader rejects the plan, and the scheduler must recalculate placement based on updated cluster data.[23, 24] To minimize latency stalls from Raft log replication, Nomad overlaps plan verification with the application phase.[24] While waiting for Plan N to be applied to the Raft log, the planner begins verifying Plan N+1 against an optimistic state snapshot (StateSnapshot via snapshotMinIndex), assuming the previous transaction will succeed.[24] Administrators can monitor scheduling and dry-run deployments using Nomad's CLI tools: Evaluation Monitoring: The nomad eval status command, with flags like -monitor and -verbose, provides real-time logs and displays plan annotations from the scheduler's reconciliation stage.[25] Dry-Run Planning: The nomad job plan command runs a dry-run of the scheduler.[26] It returns a structured diff between the local and remote job configurations and outputs a job modify index.[26] This index can be enforced using nomad job run -check-index to prevent collision modifications if another administrator has updated the job since the plan was run.[26] The nomad job plan command returns specific exit codes: 0: No allocations created or destroyed.[26] 1: Allocations created or destroyed.[26] 255: Error determining plan results.[26] 2.3. Multi-Workload Driver Execution and Resource Isolation Nomad's task driver architecture allows it to run diverse workloads, including virtual machines, raw binaries, and containers.[22, 27] Task drivers integrate with the host operating system to execute tasks and provide resource isolation.[22] The characteristics of two non-containerized drivers demonstrate the operational range of this model: The resource isolation mechanisms of these drivers differ significantly: QEMU Driver (qemu): This driver provides virtual machine execution by running guest OS images (such as .qcow2, .img, or .iso) inside a QEMU emulator.[28] By utilizing the KVM kernel module, it achieves hardware-assisted virtualization and performance.[28] The hypervisor provides strong, kernel-level resource isolation, making it suitable for untrusted workloads or legacy systems requiring a dedicated OS.[28] Network isolation, however, defaults to the host namespace, requiring port mapping unless a custom CNI is configured.[28] Raw Fork/Exec Driver (raw_exec): This driver executes commands directly on the host without filesystem or network isolation.[29, 30] Tasks run under the same user account as the Nomad client itself, making it highly powerful but introducing significant security risks.[27, 29] It is disabled by default and used for legacy applications or workloads requiring raw, unconstrained access to host hardware.[27, 29] Process tree leaks can be prevented by running Nomad with appropriate privileges on Linux to leverage cgroups, ensuring that child process trees are reaped when the allocation terminates.[29] Additionally, when memory oversubscription is enabled in the cluster, tasks using the raw_exec driver can configure uncapped memory utilization by setting memory_max = -1, preventing Nomad from terminating the task if its memory footprint spikes beyond the scheduled limits.[27, 29] 2.4. Infrastructure Integration and License Implications Nomad is designed to integrate with HashiCorp's broader infrastructure suite: Consul Integration: Provides automatic clustering (discovering peers when Consul agents are co-located), dynamic service discovery, and Consul service mesh transparent proxy routing.[31] In addition, Nomad uses Consul Template in the task's template block to dynamically regenerate configuration files when Consul Key-Value pairs or Vault secrets change.[31] Vault Integration: Allows Nomad servers to securely acquire a Vault token on behalf of tasks.[32] Nomad clients distribute these tokens to individual tasks, allowing applications to retrieve secrets dynamically.[32] Enterprise Licensing: Nomad Enterprise features can be activated by providing a license string or a path to a license file via environment variables like NOMAD_LICENSE or NOMAD_LICENSE_PATH.[33] However, Nomad's ecosystem has faced scrutiny due to HashiCorp's transition from the open-source Mozilla Public License (MPL 2.0) to the restrictive Business Source License (BSL v1.1) for all releases following Nomad 1.6.1, Consul 1.16.1, and Vault 1.14.1.[34, 35] The BSL v1.1 is a "source-available" license that allows free non-production use and production use, unless the application is hosted or embedded within a commercial offering that competes with HashiCorp's products.[35] Because the definition of a "competitive offering" is broad and subject to shift over time, this licensing change has introduced compliance and legal concerns for enterprises.[8, 35] This has led some organizations to avoid BSL-licensed infrastructure, driving adoption toward CNCF alternatives like k3s.[8, 35]
